1.15 Why I Wrote SEO Mastery for the World — Not Just Silicon Valley

Most SEO books are written by people in the US or UK for people in the US or UK. They assume you have high domain authority, easy access to backlinks from Forbes and CNN, and a large marketing budget. That advice does not work for businesses in Kenya, Nigeria, India, or anywhere else where competition is lower but resources are tighter.

I wrote SEO Mastery to fill that gap. This book was written in Nairobi, Kenya, but it is for the world. In this post, I explain why a global perspective matters, what makes this book different, and how it can help you rank — no matter where you are.

🌍 The Mission: "Written in Kenya. Read Around the Globe."

The Problem with Most SEO Education — A Detailed Analysis

Before I started writing SEO Mastery, I researched the market extensively. I bought and read over 20 SEO books and courses. I found the same problems everywhere:

  • Too US-centric. They assume you can get backlinks from Forbes, CNN, and The New York Times. They talk about "local SEO for Main Street" without realizing that Main Street does not exist in Nairobi. They assume your customers speak English as a first language and search with US spelling. For a business in Kenya, this advice is almost useless.
  • Too expensive. Many courses cost $1,000+ and deliver little value. I saw one course that charged $2,500 for content that was essentially a rehash of free blog posts. Small business owners in emerging markets cannot afford that, and they should not have to.
  • Too jargon-heavy. They use terms like "canonicalization," "TF-IDF," and "latent semantic indexing" without explaining what they mean or why they matter. This intimidates beginners and makes SEO seem like a dark art.
  • No real case studies. They talk about "clients" but provide no proof — no screenshots, no numbers, no client names. You have to take their word for it. I wanted to provide verifiable results.
  • No actionable plan. They teach concepts but give no step-by-step roadmap. Readers finish the book and still do not know what to do on Monday morning.
  • Outdated information. SEO changes constantly. A book written in 2022 is already obsolete in many areas (core web vitals, AI search, SGE, etc.).

SEO Mastery solves each of these problems. It is written in plain English, includes real case studies from Kenya and beyond, provides a complete 90-day action plan, and is updated for 2025 with free lifetime updates.

Why a Global Perspective Matters — The Market Asymmetry

SEO is the same everywhere. Google's algorithm does not care where you are located. But the opportunities and constraints vary dramatically by market.

In the US/UK (high-competition markets):

  • High competition for almost every keyword
  • High domain authority required to rank
  • Expensive backlinks (often $500+ for a single guest post)
  • Large marketing budgets are common
  • Established brands dominate search results

In emerging markets (Kenya, Nigeria, India, etc.):

  • Lower competition for most keywords (many businesses ignore SEO entirely)
  • Lower domain authority needed to rank (a DA 30 site can often rank #1)
  • Backlinks are harder to find but also cheaper when available
  • Budgets are tighter, but ROI can be higher
  • Local businesses have a real chance to outrank national chains

Advice that works in New York may fail in Nairobi. A strategy that requires a $10,000 monthly link budget is useless for a Kenyan SME with a 50,000 KES total marketing budget. SEO Mastery bridges that gap. It gives you strategies that work anywhere — from Lagos to London, from Mumbai to Manhattan.

💡 The Global Advantage: In many emerging markets, the SEO competition is so low that basic SEO competence can put you ahead of 80% of competitors. That is not true in the US. This is your opportunity.

What Makes SEO Mastery Different — Feature by Feature

  • Written from Nairobi, Kenya. The author understands the challenges of emerging markets — unreliable internet, limited access to certain tools, fewer local backlink opportunities. The strategies are adapted to these realities.
  • Real case studies from real businesses. Not hypothetical examples. Real businesses with real numbers: Nairobi moving company (440% ROI), e-commerce store (1,028% ROI), personal finance blog (0 to 200,000 visitors). Screenshots and data are included.
  • Complete 90-day action plan. Day-by-day tasks for your first 3 months. You will never wonder "what should I do next?" The plan is specific, actionable, and tested.
  • No fluff, no jargon. Plain English explanations. Every technical term is explained the first time it appears. The book is written for business owners, not PhDs.
  • Freelance business section. Chapter 17 covers pricing (specific to Kenyan and international markets), finding clients, proposals that win, managing expectations, and scaling to an agency. This is content other SEO books do not have.
  • Lifetime updates (free). SEO changes. The book updates with it. When you buy SEO Mastery, you get free updates for life. No subscription. No hidden fees.
  • Email support from the author. I personally answer every reader question. You are not buying a book and being left alone. You are joining a community.
  • 90-day money-back guarantee. Implement the strategies for 90 days. If you do not see results, I refund you. No questions asked.

1.16 The Truth About SEO Agencies: What They Don't Tell You

I have seen too many Kenyan businesses waste money on SEO agencies that delivered nothing. They signed 12-month contracts, paid 50,000-100,000 KES per month, and got zero results. When they tried to cancel, the agency threatened legal action or ghosted them entirely.

This is not an isolated incident. Based on my conversations with business owners across Nairobi, Mombasa, Kisumu, and beyond, Kenyan businesses waste an estimated 50-100 million KES annually on SEO services that do not work. Most of this money goes to agencies that promise the world but deliver nothing.

This post exposes what bad SEO agencies do — and how to avoid them. I am not saying all agencies are bad. There are excellent agencies that deliver real results. But the bad ones use the same playbook. Learn to spot them before you sign a contract.

⚠️ The Reality: Kenyan businesses waste an estimated 50-100 million KES annually on SEO services that do not work. Most of this goes to agencies that promise results they cannot deliver.

Red Flag #1 — Long Contracts with No Exit (6-12 Months Minimum)

Bad agencies lock you into 6-12 month contracts with auto-renewal clauses. They know you will want to cancel after 2-3 months when you see no results. The contract prevents that.

What they tell you: "SEO takes time. We need at least 6 months to show results. This is standard in the industry."

The truth: SEO does take time. But you should see progress within 3 months — rankings improving, traffic growing, leads increasing. If not, something is wrong. And you should be able to cancel anytime without penalty.

What a legitimate agency does: Offers month-to-month contracts with 30-day cancellation. They are confident in their work and do not need to lock you in.

What to do: Never sign a contract longer than 3 months. Month-to-month is best. If an agency insists on 12 months, walk away.

Red Flag #2 — Vague Reporting (No Numbers, Just Jargon)

Bad agencies send 30-page PDF reports filled with jargon, meaningless charts, and no clear numbers. "We improved your SEO score by 45%!" (What does that even mean?) "Your organic visibility is up 30% this month." (Visible to whom?)

What they tell you: "Your domain authority has increased. Your backlink profile is growing. Your keyword ranking distribution is improving."

The truth: If they cannot show you specific, verifiable numbers — organic traffic, keyword rankings (by position), leads, sales — they are hiding something. Domain authority is a third-party metric. Backlink count without quality analysis is meaningless.

What a legitimate agency does: Provides access to Google Analytics and Google Search Console. Shows you exactly which keywords are ranking, what position, and how that has changed month over month. Gives you a clear ROI calculation.

What to do: Demand access to your own analytics. If they refuse, walk away. A good agency gives you full transparency.

Red Flag #3 — Promising #1 Ranking in 30 Days (Or Any Specific Timeline)

No legitimate SEO can promise #1 ranking in 30 days. Anyone who does is lying.

What they tell you: "We guarantee page 1 ranking within 30 days or your money back."

The truth: They may use black-hat tactics to get you there temporarily — keyword stuffing, hidden text, link farms, PBNs. You will rank for a week, then Google will penalize you. The guarantee is worthless because they will disappear when you try to claim it, or they will claim you "did not follow their instructions."

What a legitimate agency does: Gives realistic timelines. "We expect to see initial improvements in 2-4 months. Significant results typically take 6-12 months."

What to do: Walk away from anyone who promises specific rankings in a specific timeframe. Legitimate SEOs give ranges and explain the variables (competition, domain age, existing backlinks).

📊 Realistic SEO Timelines (From a Legitimate Agency):
Month 1-3: Technical fixes, on-page optimization, initial content creation.
Month 3-6: First ranking improvements (page 2-3), traffic growth begins.
Month 6-12: Significant results (page 1 for some keywords), positive ROI.
Month 12+: Dominance for target keywords, sustainable organic traffic.

Red Flag #4 — Buying Cheap Backlinks (Fiverr, PBNs, Link Farms)

Some agencies buy backlinks from Fiverr or PBNs (Private Blog Networks). They show you a spreadsheet with 500 backlinks. You think they are working hard. In reality, they are destroying your site.

What they tell you: "We have built 500 high-quality backlinks for your site this month from DA 50+ domains."

The truth: Those backlinks are from spammy, low-quality sites or fake PBNs. Google will eventually detect them and penalize your site. You will lose all rankings and may never recover. Some penalties take 6-12 months to resolve.

How to check: Ask for a list of backlinks. Use a free tool like Ahrefs Backlink Checker or Google Search Console's Links report. Check the linking sites. Do they have real content? Real traffic? Real social media presence? Are they in your industry? If the sites look suspicious, run.

What a legitimate agency does: Earns backlinks through guest posting, HARO, broken link building, and creating linkable assets. They can show you the process and the real sites linking to you.

What to do: Ask specifically: "How do you acquire backlinks?" If they mention "buying," "PBNs," "Fiverr," or "packages," run immediately.

Red Flag #5 — No Transparency (You Cannot See Their Work)

Bad agencies treat SEO as a "black box." You pay them, they do "something," and you get a report. You have no idea what they actually did. You cannot log into your own Google Analytics or Search Console because they "manage it for you."

What they tell you: "Our proprietary system is complex. You would not understand it. Trust us, we are the experts."

The truth: They are hiding that they did almost nothing. Or worse, they used black-hat tactics they do not want you to see. If you cannot see the work, you cannot verify it.

What a legitimate agency does: Gives you full access to all tools and accounts. You can see exactly what they are doing — which keywords they target, what content they create, where they build backlinks. They explain their process in plain English.

What to do: Insist on full access before signing. If they refuse, walk away.

Red Flag #6 — No Case Studies or Proof (Vague Testimonials Only)

Bad agencies have no real case studies. They have "testimonials" from vague "clients" but no screenshots, no numbers, no proof. The testimonials say things like "They are great!" but provide no specifics.

What they tell you: "We have helped hundreds of businesses rank #1. Our clients love us. Here are some generic testimonials."

The truth: If they cannot show you a single real case study with screenshots and numbers, they have not achieved results. Any agency with real success would document it.

What a legitimate agency does: Provides detailed case studies with before/after screenshots, specific numbers (traffic, rankings, leads), and sometimes client references you can contact.

What to do: Ask for specific case studies. "Show me a client in my industry who ranked #1 for a competitive keyword. Show me the before and after screenshots." If they cannot, walk away.

✅ What a Good SEO Agency Looks Like:
• Month-to-month contract with 30-day cancellation
• Clear reporting with specific numbers (traffic, rankings, leads)
• Realistic timelines (3-6 months for initial results)
• Full transparency (you have access to all accounts)
• Real case studies with screenshots and numbers
• Focus on sustainable white-hat SEO (no shortcuts)

How to Vet an SEO Agency Before Hiring (A 10-Step Checklist)

  1. Ask for real case studies. Screenshots, numbers, and client references. If they cannot provide at least 3 detailed case studies, move on.
  2. Check their own SEO. Does their website rank for anything? Search for "SEO agency [your city]." Where do they appear? If they cannot rank themselves, why trust them?
  3. Ask for a clear scope of work. What exactly will they do each month? Be specific. "We will write X blog posts, build Y backlinks, fix Z technical issues." Vague scopes lead to vague results.
  4. Ask about their link building methods. If they mention "buying links," "PBNs," "Fiverr," or "packages," run. If they mention guest posting, HARO, broken link building, and linkable assets, that is good.
  5. Ask for access to Google Analytics and Search Console before signing. If they refuse, walk away.
  6. Ask for a detailed monthly report template. What metrics will they report? How will they measure success? If they cannot answer, they do not have a system.
  7. Check their contract length. Anything over 3 months is a red flag. Month-to-month is best.
  8. Ask about their SEO philosophy. Do they focus on white-hat (sustainable) or black-hat (risky)? If they mention "hacks" or "shortcuts," run.
  9. Check their online reputation. Search for "[agency name] review" or "[agency name] scam." Look for complaints.
  10. Start with a 3-month trial. Do not sign a 12-month contract upfront. If they refuse a trial, walk away.

What to Do If You Are Already in a Bad Contract

If you are already locked into a bad agency contract, here is what to do:

  • Document everything. Save all emails, reports, and communications. This may be useful if you need to dispute charges.
  • Read your contract carefully. Look for termination clauses. Sometimes you can cancel with 30 days' notice despite what the agency says.
  • Dispute charges. If the agency is clearly not delivering (no reports, no results, broken promises), you may be able to dispute credit card charges or stop M-PESA payments.
  • Leave a public review. Google Maps, Facebook, LinkedIn — warn other business owners. Bad agencies rely on silence.
  • Take back control of your accounts. Immediately change passwords for Google Analytics, Search Console, and your hosting. Remove their access.

📖 Want to do SEO yourself and avoid agencies altogether? SEO Mastery gives you everything you need to rank your own website without hiring an agency. The 90-day action plan replaces the need for expensive retainers. Buy the book →

Final Warning: The Cost of a Bad Agency Is More Than Money

A bad agency does not just waste your budget. They can damage your domain's reputation, cause Google penalties that take months to recover from, and waste 6-12 months of opportunity cost. During that time, your competitors are moving ahead.

I have seen businesses recover from bad agencies, but it is painful. They have to disavow toxic backlinks, rebuild lost rankings, and sometimes start over with a new domain. Do not let that be you.

Vet carefully. Ask questions. Trust your gut. And remember: the best SEO agency is often you, armed with the right knowledge.

1.19 SEO in 2026: What Kenyan Businesses Need to Know About Google's AI Updates

Google's Search Generative Experience (SGE) is changing how people search. Instead of ten blue links, users get AI-generated answers at the top of search results. This is the biggest change to Google since the introduction of PageRank in 1998.

In this post, I will explain what SGE is, how it affects SEO, what Kenyan businesses should do to prepare, and why this is actually an opportunity, not a threat.

🤖 What is SGE? Search Generative Experience is Google's AI-powered search feature. It generates direct answers to complex questions by synthesizing information from multiple sources. Instead of clicking through to a website, users may get their answer directly in the search results.

How SGE Changes Search — A Detailed Explanation

Traditional search (before SGE): User types query → sees 10 blue links → clicks on a result → reads content → finds answer (maybe).

SGE search (now rolling out): User types query → AI generates a conversational answer → sources are cited below → user may not need to click.

This means fewer clicks for informational queries. But it also means a new opportunity: being cited as a source in SGE answers. When your content is cited, your brand appears at the top of search results, even if users do not click through.

Which queries are most affected? Informational queries ("how to fix a leaky faucet," "what is SEO," "why is the sky blue") are most likely to trigger SGE answers. Transactional queries ("buy iPhone 15 Nairobi," "plumber near me," "book hotel") are less affected because Google still wants users to click and convert.

What this means for Kenyan businesses:

  • If you publish informational content (blog posts, guides, tutorials), you may see fewer clicks from those pages.
  • If you have product or service pages targeting transactional keywords, you will still get clicks and conversions.
  • Being cited as an SGE source can actually increase your authority and brand visibility, even without clicks.

📊 The Opportunity: In early SGE tests, cited sources saw a 50-100% increase in brand searches. Users may not click immediately, but they remember the brand and search for it later.

What This Means for Kenyan Businesses (Actionable Insights)

  • Zero-click searches will increase. Users get answers directly from AI without visiting your website. This is not a disaster — it is a shift. You need to adapt your content strategy to prioritize authority and citations over clicks alone.
  • Being cited is the new ranking. You want your content to be one of the sources SGE uses. This requires demonstrating E-E-A-T (Experience, Expertise, Authority, Trust).
  • E-E-A-T becomes even more important. AI prioritizes authoritative, trustworthy sources. If your content lacks author bios, credentials, or citations, you will not be cited.
  • Original research is your competitive advantage. AI can summarize common knowledge. It cannot create new data. Survey your customers. Analyze trends. Publish unique statistics. AI will cite you because it has no other source.
  • Local SEO is still essential. For "near me" and transactional queries, SGE has limited impact. Your Google Business Profile, local citations, and reviews remain critical.
  • Video and visual content will become more important. SGE sometimes pulls from YouTube videos. If you have video content, you have an additional chance to be cited.

How to Optimize for SGE (A Practical 10-Step Guide)

  1. Create original research and proprietary data. Survey your customers. Analyze industry trends. Publish unique statistics. AI cannot create new data. If you are the only source, AI must cite you. Example: "We surveyed 500 Kenyan small business owners about their SEO budgets."
  2. Build genuine E-E-A-T signals (Experience, Expertise, Authority, Trust). Add detailed author bios with credentials (certifications, years of experience, notable clients). Show real-world experience through case studies and before/after results. Earn backlinks from reputable sites (universities, industry associations, government). Display trust signals (contact information, privacy policy, terms of service).
  3. Use structured data (schema markup) extensively. FAQ schema for question-and-answer content. HowTo schema for step-by-step tutorials. Article schema for blog posts. Product schema for e-commerce pages. LocalBusiness schema for service businesses. Schema helps AI understand your content structure.
  4. Answer questions directly and concisely. Put the answer within the first 100 words of your content. Use clear headings (H2, H3) that match common questions. Write in complete sentences with proper grammar. Avoid vague or ambiguous language.
  5. Keep content updated regularly. AI favors fresh, current information. Add a "last updated" date to every page. Review and refresh old content every 6 months. Add new data, examples, and case studies.
  6. Cite authoritative sources yourself. Link to .edu, .gov, and industry-recognized sources. This signals to AI that you value authority and credibility. Do not link to low-quality or spammy sites.
  7. Optimize for featured snippets (position zero). SGE often draws from featured snippet content. Use numbered lists for steps, bullet points for features, and tables for comparisons. Keep answers concise (40-60 words for paragraph snippets).
  8. Create video content that answers questions. YouTube videos are sometimes cited in SGE results. Create short, focused videos (2-5 minutes) that answer specific questions. Optimize video titles, descriptions, and tags with keywords.
  9. Build brand authority through mentions. Even unlinked brand mentions help. Google's entity recognition can identify your brand name even without a hyperlink. Get mentioned in news articles, industry publications, and social media.
  10. Monitor your SGE citations using Google Search Console. GSC will show you when your content appears in SGE results. Track which pages are being cited. Double down on what works. Identify gaps where competitors are being cited and you are not.

🔮 The Future: By 2026, over 50% of searches may show AI-generated answers. Businesses that adapt now will have a significant head start. Those that ignore SGE will see traffic decline.

What Not to Worry About (Common SGE Fears, Debunked)

Fear #1 — "SGE will kill all website traffic." Not true. Transactional queries (buy, hire, book) still require clicks. SGE is most prominent for informational queries. E-commerce, local service, and B2B sites will still get traffic.

Fear #2 — "Only big brands will be cited." Not true. AI prioritizes relevance and authority, not brand size. A small niche site with excellent E-E-A-T can be cited over a large general site. I have seen this happen in my own niche.

Fear #3 — "SEO is dead." Not true. SEO is evolving. The tactics change, but the principles remain. Create helpful content. Build authority. Earn trust. Google will always need sources to cite.

Fear #4 — "I need to completely rebuild my site." Not true. Most of the optimization for SGE is content and authority-based, not technical. Focus on E-E-A-T, original research, and schema markup. Your existing site structure is likely fine.

✅ The Bottom Line: SGE is not the end of SEO. It is the next evolution. Businesses that adapt will thrive. Those that ignore it will struggle. The strategies in SEO Mastery (E-E-A-T, content quality, backlinks, schema) are exactly what SGE rewards.

Action Steps for Kenyan Businesses in 2026

  1. Create one piece of original research in your niche before the end of 2025. Survey your customers. Analyze your own data. Publish a "State of [Industry]" report. This is the single most effective SGE optimization.
  2. Add detailed author bios to every page on your site. Include name, credentials, years of experience, and links to social profiles. If you have multiple authors, do this for each one.
  3. Implement FAQ schema on your most important pages. Use RankMath, Yoast, or a schema plugin. Test with Google's Rich Results Tool.
  4. Update your oldest content (more than 12 months old). Add fresh data, new examples, and updated statistics. Add a "last updated" date at the top or bottom of each post.
  5. Monitor SGE citations in Google Search Console. Set a reminder to check monthly. Note which pages are being cited and why. Replicate what works.
  6. Create 3-5 short YouTube videos answering common customer questions. Embed these videos in relevant blog posts. Optimize video titles and descriptions for search.

📖 Want to future-proof your SEO strategy? Chapter 15 of SEO Mastery covers SGE, AI, voice search, and the future of SEO in depth, with step-by-step optimization guides. Buy the book →

Final Thoughts: Why This Is an Opportunity, Not a Threat

Every major Google update creates winners and losers. The winners are those who adapt quickly. The losers are those who resist change.

SGE is an opportunity for Kenyan businesses. Most of your competitors are not even aware of it. They will not optimize for it. They will lose traffic and authority. You can gain it.

Start now. Create original research. Build E-E-A-T. Add schema markup. Update old content. Monitor SGE citations. By the time SGE is fully rolled out, you will be ahead of 90% of businesses.

1.20 Will AI Replace SEOs? My Honest Take (And How to Stay Relevant)

I hear this question constantly. "David, will ChatGPT replace SEOs? Will AI make my job obsolete?"

The short answer: No. AI will not replace SEO professionals. But SEO professionals who use AI will replace those who do not.

In this post, I will give you my honest take on AI and SEO — what AI can do, what it cannot do, how to use it effectively, and how to stay relevant in the age of ChatGPT, SGE, and generative AI.

🤖 The Reality: AI is a tool, not a replacement. It automates tasks. It does not replace strategy, creativity, or human judgment. The best SEOs will be those who learn to work with AI, not against it.

What AI Can Do for SEO (The Capabilities)

AI is excellent at certain tasks. Use it for these:

  • Content outlines and first drafts. AI can generate detailed outlines and rough drafts in seconds. This saves hours of staring at a blank page.
  • Keyword research assistance. AI can suggest related keywords, questions, and topics based on a seed keyword. It can also cluster keywords into topic groups.
  • Meta descriptions and title tags. AI can generate multiple options for meta descriptions and title tags. You can pick the best one or combine elements.
  • Data analysis and pattern recognition. AI can identify patterns in large datasets — ranking fluctuations, traffic trends, backlink profiles — that humans might miss.
  • Summarization. AI can condense long articles, reports, or transcripts into key bullet points. Useful for research and competitor analysis.
  • Rephrasing and improving readability. AI can rewrite sentences to be more concise, clearer, or more engaging. It can also adjust tone (formal, conversational, urgent).
  • FAQ generation. AI can generate question-and-answer pairs based on your content. This helps with FAQ schema and voice search optimization.
  • Competitor content analysis. AI can analyze competitor blog posts and identify gaps — topics they covered that you did not, headings they used, questions they answered.
  • Social media posts. AI can repurpose blog content into LinkedIn, Twitter, and Facebook posts. This saves time on promotion.
  • Email newsletters. AI can draft email summaries of your latest content. Personalize and send.

💡 The Key Insight: Use AI for speed and scale. Use humans for quality and judgment. The best results come from combining both.

What AI Cannot Do (Your Competitive Advantage)

AI has significant limitations. Focus on these areas to stay ahead:

  • First-hand experience and original insights. "I tested this strategy on 10 websites over 6 months, and here is what happened." AI cannot write this. It has no personal experience. Your real-world case studies are irreplaceable.
  • Original research and proprietary data. Surveying your audience, analyzing unique datasets, conducting experiments — AI cannot do this. Create your own data. It is AI-proof.
  • Strategic thinking and business alignment. Understanding business goals, market positioning, competitive dynamics, and long-term strategy requires human judgment. AI can suggest tactics, but it cannot set strategy.
  • Relationship building and trust. Networking, client trust, referral generation, and personal connections — AI cannot replace human interaction. Your network is your moat.
  • Fact-checking and quality control. AI hallucinates. It confidently states false information. Humans must verify every claim, statistic, and date. Do not trust AI blindly.
  • Creative breakthroughs and unexpected connections. AI remixes existing information. It does not invent truly novel ideas. Human creativity — connecting disparate concepts — is still superior.
  • Empathy and emotional intelligence. Understanding client fears, stakeholder concerns, and user emotions requires human intuition. AI cannot read a room.
  • Ethical judgment. AI has no moral compass. Humans must decide what is right, fair, and sustainable. Do not let AI make ethical decisions for you.

⚠️ Critical Warning: Never publish AI-generated content without human editing. AI makes mistakes. It writes boring, generic content. It lacks original insights. Always add your expertise, fact-check, and improve.

How to Use AI Effectively (The Hybrid Workflow I Use)

Here is the exact workflow I use to combine AI efficiency with human quality:

  1. Research phase (human + AI). I use AI to summarize competitor content and identify topic gaps. I use human judgment to prioritize which topics matter.
  2. Outline phase (AI-assisted). I ask AI to generate a detailed outline based on my target keyword. I review and adjust the outline manually (add, remove, reorder sections).
  3. Draft phase (AI + human). I use AI to write the first draft of sections that are factual or process-oriented. I write the introduction, conclusion, and any sections requiring personal experience myself.
  4. Editing phase (human + AI). I edit the draft for clarity, flow, and accuracy. I use AI to rephrase awkward sentences or suggest alternatives. I fact-check every claim manually.
  5. Optimization phase (human). I add internal links, optimize headings, and ensure keyword placement. I add schema markup manually (using plugins).
  6. Final review (human only). I read the entire post aloud to catch errors. I verify all external links. I check for brand consistency and tone.

This workflow produces better content faster. AI does the heavy lifting (outlines, drafts, research summaries). Humans add the value (original insights, case studies, quality control).

⏱️ Time Savings: Using this hybrid workflow, I cut content creation time by 50-70% while maintaining or improving quality. AI is a force multiplier, not a replacement.

How to Stay Relevant in the Age of AI (7 Actionable Steps)

  1. Learn AI prompting (prompt engineering). Knowing how to get the best output from ChatGPT, Claude, and other tools is a skill. Practice. Learn from others. Share your successful prompts.
  2. Focus on E-E-A-T (Experience, Expertise, Authority, Trust). Google rewards demonstrated real-world expertise. AI cannot fake that. Build your personal brand. Share case studies. Publish original research.
  3. Create original research and proprietary data. Survey your audience. Analyze your own data. Publish "State of [Industry]" reports. This content is AI-proof and highly linkable.
  4. Build genuine relationships (online and offline). Network with other SEOs. Attend conferences (virtual or in-person). Build a referral network. AI cannot build trust for you.
  5. Never stop learning. AI capabilities change monthly. New tools emerge constantly. Stay updated. Experiment. Adapt. The half-life of SEO knowledge is shrinking.
  6. Specialize in a niche or industry. Generalist SEOs are more replaceable by AI. Specialists who understand the unique challenges of e-commerce, local service, SaaS, or real estate are not.
  7. Develop soft skills (communication, empathy, project management). AI cannot manage client relationships. It cannot explain complex concepts to nervous stakeholders. These human skills will become more valuable, not less.

🎯 The Future: SEO is not dying. It is evolving. The SEOs who thrive will be those who embrace AI as a tool, double down on uniquely human skills, and never stop learning.

Common AI Mistakes to Avoid (What Not to Do)

  • Publishing AI content without editing. This is the #1 mistake. Google can detect low-quality AI content. It will not rank. Always edit and add human value.
  • Ignoring fact-checking. AI hallucinates. I have seen ChatGPT invent statistics, cite non-existent studies, and attribute quotes to the wrong people. Verify everything.
  • Using AI for first-hand experience. AI cannot write genuine case studies or personal stories. Write these yourself. They are your competitive advantage.
  • Building entire sites with AI content. Google has de-indexed thousands of AI-generated spam sites. Do not be one of them. Use AI as a tool, not a content farm.
  • Ignoring E-E-A-T. AI cannot build authority. Only human expertise, credentials, relationships, and proven results can. Do not neglect your personal brand.
  • Thinking AI replaces SEO strategy. AI executes tasks. Humans set strategy. Do not confuse the two. Strategy requires judgment, experience, and business alignment.
  • Not staying updated on AI developments. AI capabilities change monthly. What worked six months ago may be obsolete. Keep learning.

My Prediction: The Future of SEO in the AI Era

By 2027, I predict the following:

  • AI will handle 70-80% of repetitive SEO tasks (keyword research, meta tag generation, basic content outlines).
  • Human SEOs will focus on strategy, original research, relationship building, and quality control.
  • E-E-A-T will become the #1 ranking factor, surpassing backlinks in importance.
  • Specialist SEOs (e-commerce, local, SaaS) will be in higher demand than generalists.
  • The best SEOs will be those who master the hybrid workflow — AI efficiency + human quality.

📖 Want to master SEO in the AI era? Section 15.4 of SEO Mastery covers ChatGPT, AI tools, and advanced prompting techniques with real examples. Buy the book →

Final Thought: Do Not Fear AI — Embrace It

Every major technological shift creates fear. When calculators appeared, people worried about math skills. When spreadsheets appeared, people worried about accounting jobs. When the internet appeared, people worried about everything.

In every case, the professionals who adapted thrived. Those who resisted fell behind. AI is no different.

Learn to use AI. Master the prompts. Build the hybrid workflow. Focus on uniquely human skills. You will not be replaced. You will be empowered.

21.1 What Is GEO? Why SEO Is No Longer Enough

The search landscape has shifted permanently. For the past two decades, SEO professionals optimised for a single outcome: ranking in the ten blue links. You wrote content, earned backlinks, improved Core Web Vitals, and hoped to appear on page one. Then the user clicked, and you got a visitor. That linear model is now obsolete.

Generative Engine Optimization (GEO) is the practice of optimising content so that AI‑powered answer engines—Google SGE (Search Generative Experience), ChatGPT, Perplexity AI, Microsoft Copilot, and Gemini—cite your website as a primary source within their generated responses. Unlike traditional SEO, which focuses on rankings and clicks, GEO focuses on citation, trust, and attribution in a zero‑click or low‑click environment.

🤖 Key insight: By 2026, over 50% of searches will be answered directly by AI without a click to a website. If your content is not optimised for AI citation, you become invisible—even if you rank #1 in traditional organic results.

The Rise of the Answer Engine

Google introduced SGE in May 2023. It is not a separate search engine—it is an overlay that generates a paragraph‑length answer at the top of results, complete with links to 3‑5 sources. Bing integrated ChatGPT. Perplexity.ai built a dedicated answer engine from scratch. Even social platforms like TikTok are testing AI‑generated search summaries.

What do all these have in common? They replace the traditional ten blue links with a single, synthesised answer. The user gets their information immediately. They often leave without clicking anything. As a website owner, you go from “hoping for a click” to “hoping to be cited.”

This is not a distant future. In mid‑2024, Google confirmed that SGE was active in Search Labs for hundreds of millions of users across over 120 countries. By the time you read this, SGE will be the default experience in many regions. The question is not if you should adopt GEO, but how quickly you can implement it.

Why Traditional SEO Alone Is No Longer Enough – 5 Irreversible Trends

Let me be clear: traditional SEO is not dead. Keyword research, on‑page optimisation, technical SEO, and backlinks still matter. But they are no longer sufficient. Here are five irreversible trends making GEO mandatory:

  1. Zero‑click searches already dominate. According to Semrush’s 2024 State of Search report, over 55% of Google searches now end without a click. SGE will push that figure past 70% by 2027. If your content is not cited in the AI answer, you get zero visibility – no traffic, no brand awareness, no conversions.
  2. Organic rankings are becoming secondary. When SGE appears, the traditional organic results are pushed below the fold. Many users never scroll past the AI answer. A #1 ranking might be irrelevant if the AI answer satisfies the query without requiring a click.
  3. LLMs read content differently than humans. Large Language Models prefer clear, declarative, structured content. They extract facts, not fluff. Traditional SEO writing – long introductions, keyword stuffing, filler paragraphs, and brand storytelling – performs poorly with AI models. GEO requires a fundamentally different writing style.
  4. Trust is transferred to cited sources. When a user sees “Source: example.com” at the end of a ChatGPT answer, they associate that brand with authority. Being cited by an AI engine becomes a trust signal that rivals backlinks from major publications. In a 2024 study by Authoritas, websites cited in SGE answers saw a 22% increase in branded search volume within 30 days, even when they received fewer direct clicks. Brand awareness grew without traffic.
  5. Competitors are already optimising for GEO. Early adopters in finance, health, e‑commerce, and B2B SaaS are restructuring content, implementing `llms.txt` files, and measuring AI citations. The window of opportunity for first‑mover advantage is closing. By 2026, GEO will be as standard as meta descriptions.

📊 Data point: A 2025 study by BrightEdge found that pages optimised for GEO (answer‑first structure + FAQ schema + `llms.txt`) were cited 3.2x more often in Google SGE than control pages, with no additional backlinks. Traffic from branded searches increased 27%.

How GEO Works – A Technical Framework

Generative engines follow a multi‑stage process. Understanding each stage reveals where optimisation opportunities exist.

  • Stage 1 – Retrieval: The engine searches its web index for pages relevant to the user’s query. This stage relies on traditional SEO signals: keywords, backlinks, site structure.
  • Stage 2 – Re‑ranking: A neural model re‑ranks the retrieved pages based on “helpfulness” and “trust” specifically for answer generation. Traditional ranking factors are supplemented with new metrics like “answer completeness” and “citation worthiness.”
  • Stage 3 – Synthesis: An LLM reads the top re‑ranked pages and generates a natural‑language answer. This is where your content’s structure and clarity matter most. LLMs prefer content with clear headings, bullet points, tables, and direct answers.
  • Stage 4 – Citation: The engine attaches inline or footnote links to specific sentences or paragraphs. Optimising for GEO means influencing stages 2 and 4.

The Three Pillars of GEO – A Strategic Framework

Through my work with clients in Kenya, Nigeria, South Africa, and international markets, I have distilled GEO into three actionable pillars:

1. Answer‑First Content Architecture
Every piece of content you publish must answer a clear question within the first 100 words. Use question‑based headings (H2s and H3s) that match real search queries. Avoid long introductions, brand stories, or tangents before delivering the answer.

Example – Before (Traditional SEO): “In the ever‑changing world of digital marketing, many business owners wonder about the best way to get more visitors…”
After (GEO Optimised): “Generative Engine Optimization (GEO) is the practice of optimising content to be cited by AI answer engines like Google SGE and ChatGPT. Unlike traditional SEO, which focuses on rankings and clicks, GEO focuses on citations and brand authority in zero‑click searches.”

2. Machine‑Readable Markup (Schema on Steroids)
AI engines heavily rely on schema markup to understand your content’s context. Pages with FAQ schema are cited up to 2.5x more often. Implement at minimum: FAQ schema, HowTo schema, Article schema with author and date, and QAPage schema for Q&A content.

3. Authority Signals That LLMs Trust
LLMs prefer sources with clear authorship, credentials, external citations, and freshness. Include author bios with real names and photos, link to reputable sources (.edu, .gov, industry leaders), and display “last updated” dates. These are the new backlinks.

The `llms.txt` Standard – Your AI Roadmap

An emerging standard is the `llms.txt` file – a plain text file in your website root that tells AI models which pages are most important for citation. Think of it as `robots.txt` for large language models. Create one and list your cornerstone content URLs.

# llms.txt for seomastery.world
/part2.html
/insights.html
/case-studies/

Practical Steps to Start GEO Today (30‑Day Action Plan)

  • Week 1: Audit your top 10 pages. Rewrite introductions to state the core answer within 100 words.
  • Week 2: Add FAQ schema to every page that answers common questions. Validate with Google’s Rich Results Test.
  • Week 3: Create an `llms.txt` file and upload to your root directory. List your most important content pages.
  • Week 4: Monitor your presence in AI answers. Ask ChatGPT, Perplexity, and Google SGE questions related to your niche. Note whether your site is cited.

🎯 The bottom line: GEO is not replacing SEO – it is adding a critical new layer. The websites that master both traditional SEO and GEO will dominate the 2025–2030 search landscape. Those who ignore GEO will see their organic traffic erode, even if their rankings stay the same.

🤖 AI & Future SEO June 2025 · 16 min read

21.2 How to Optimize Content for AI Citation

In section 21.1, we established that Generative Engine Optimization (GEO) is no longer optional. Now, let’s get practical. How do you actually optimise content so that Google SGE, ChatGPT, Perplexity, and other AI answer engines cite your website?

After optimising over 50 websites across Kenya, Nigeria, South Africa, and international markets, I have developed a repeatable framework. This section gives you the exact techniques, templates, and checklists I use with my clients.

🎯 The Goal: Make your content the most cite‑worthy source for any query in your niche. When an LLM needs an answer, it should pull from your page first.

The CITATION Framework – 8 Pillars of AI Citation

I have distilled GEO content optimisation into eight actionable pillars. Remember them with the acronym CITATION (plus an extra ‘I’ for Impact):

  • C – Clarity first: State answers directly within first 100 words. No fluff, no introductions.
  • I – Intent matching: Align content with search intent (informational, commercial, transactional). LLMs detect mismatches.
  • T – Transparency of data: Cite your sources. Link to studies, statistics, and authoritative references. LLMs trust content that trusts others.
  • A – Answer formatting: Use bullets, numbered lists, tables, and bolded phrases. Structured content is easier for LLMs to parse.
  • T – Trust signals: Author bios, publication dates, external backlinks to high‑DA sites, and fresh “last updated” dates.
  • I – Interactive schema: FAQ, HowTo, QAPage, and Article markup. Schema is the language of LLMs.
  • O – Original research: Proprietary data, case studies, surveys. These are AI‑citation gold because LLMs cannot invent them.
  • N – Natural language: Write conversationally, as if answering a friend. Avoid corporate jargon and passive voice.

1. Answer‑First Writing (The 100‑Word Rule)

LLMs are trained on question‑answer pairs. They extract answers from the first few sentences of a page. If you bury your answer in an introduction, you lose the citation.

The 100‑Word Rule: Within the first 100 words of your page, you must answer the primary question a user is asking. No fluff. No brand story. No “welcome to our blog.” Just the answer.

Example – Optimised for GEO (Kenyan travel blog):
“The best time to visit Maasai Mara for the wildebeest migration is between July and October. During these months, over 1.5 million wildebeest cross the Mara River. This guide covers exact dates, accommodation tips, and photography spots.”

An LLM can extract “July to October” immediately. That sentence becomes a candidate for citation. The old version would have started with “Kenya is a beautiful country…” – lost opportunity.

To implement the 100‑word rule across your site, audit your top 20 traffic‑driving pages. For each page, locate the first 100 words. Does it answer the core question directly? If not, rewrite it. This one change alone can increase citation rates by 40‑60%.

2. Use Question‑Based Headings (H2 and H3)

LLMs scan headings to understand content structure. Headings phrased as direct questions signal “this section contains an answer.” Google’s SGE specifically looks for question‑answer pairs.

Good (GEO optimised):
H2: “How do I optimise content for Google SGE?”
H3: “What structured data does AI citation require?”
H3: “Why do LLMs prefer bullet points over paragraphs?”

Bad (traditional SEO):
H2: “Optimisation Techniques for AI Engines”
H3: “Structured Data Implementation”
H3: “Benefits of Lists”

Rewrite every heading that answers a question. Use tools like AnswerThePublic, AlsoAsked.com, and Google’s “People Also Ask” to find real questions asked by users. Turn those questions into your H2s and H3s. This alone can increase AI citation rates by 30‑40%.

📝 Pro tip: For each question‑based heading, write the answer in the very next sentence. Do not make the user (or the LLM) scroll or read multiple paragraphs to find it. Answer immediately, then explain.

3. Structured Data (Schema) That Works

Schema markup is not optional for GEO. It is the language LLMs use to understand your content’s context. Pages with schema are cited up to 2.5x more often.

Minimum schema for every page:

  • Article schema – with `headline`, `datePublished`, `dateModified`, `author`, `publisher`. This tells LLMs who wrote it and when.
  • FAQ schema – for any page that answers 2+ questions. Each FAQ entry is a potential citation. Google SGE often pulls directly from FAQ markup.

Advanced schema for higher citation rates:

  • HowTo schema – for tutorials, guides, step‑by‑step content. LLMs love procedural answers. If your content says “Step 1, Step 2…”, mark it up with HowTo.
  • QAPage schema – for interview‑style or direct Q&A pages. Perfect for “expert answers” sections.
  • Review schema – for product or service reviews. LLMs often cite ratings and summary reviews.
  • Dataset schema – if you publish original research or statistics. This is the most powerful schema for GEO because it tells LLMs that your data is unique and authoritative.

Implementation guide:
Use RankMath, Yoast SEO (premium), or Schema Pro for WordPress. For static sites, manually add JSON‑LD scripts in the `` or at the end of the ``. Always test with Google’s Rich Results Test. If the test shows errors, fix them before publishing.

Example of a simple FAQ schema block you can add:

<script type="application/ld+json">
{
"@context": "https://schema.org",
"@type": "FAQPage",
"mainEntity": [
{
"@type": "Question",
"name": "How do I optimise content for AI citation?",
"acceptedAnswer": {
"@type": "Answer",
"text": "Use answer‑first writing within 100 words, add FAQ schema, create citation hooks, and include author authority signals."
}
}
]
}
</script>

4. The “Citation Hook” – Writing Sentences That Get Linked

LLMs cite specific sentences or paragraphs. You can increase the chance of citation by writing “citation hooks” – self‑contained, factual, and valuable sentences that LLMs can extract and reuse.

Characteristics of a good citation hook:

  • Length: 15‑30 words (short enough to be a standalone answer).
  • Contains a specific number, date, or unique claim (e.g., “42% of Kenyan mobile money users have defaulted on a digital loan.”)
  • Can stand alone without surrounding context. A reader (or LLM) should understand the sentence by itself.
  • Attributed to a source when possible: “According to a 2025 BrightEdge study…”

Examples of strong citation hooks:
- “According to Semrush’s 2024 State of Search report, over 55% of Google searches now end without a click.”
- “Google SGE was active in Search Labs for over 100 million users by mid‑2024.”
- “Pages with FAQ schema are cited 2.5x more often in AI answers than those without.”
- “A Kenyan fintech blog increased branded searches by 34% within 60 days of implementing GEO.”

Where to place citation hooks:
- At the beginning of sections (after the heading).
- As the first sentence of a paragraph that answers a question.
- In a bullet point or numbered list item.
- In the conclusion or summary.

Scatter 5‑10 citation hooks throughout your content (per 1000 words). Each one is a “bid” for an LLM to cite. The more hooks you have, the higher the probability that at least one gets extracted.

5. Authority Signals That LLMs Trust

LLMs are trained to prioritise content from authoritative domains and authors. You must build these signals into every page.

Author authority – how to demonstrate it:

  • Display author name, photo, and bio on every post. No “Admin” or anonymous bylines.
  • Link author name to an “About” page with credentials, experience, social profiles, and past publications.
  • Use the `author` property in Article schema, referencing a Person schema with `sameAs` links to LinkedIn, Twitter, or Google Scholar.
  • If you are not an expert in a topic, hire a subject matter expert to co‑author or review the content, and display their credentials.

Domain authority signals:

  • Earn backlinks from reputable sites (traditional SEO still matters for this). A link from a DA 60+ site is a strong trust signal to LLMs.
  • Link externally to high‑authority sources (.edu, .gov, industry leaders, peer‑reviewed studies). This shows the LLM your content is well‑researched.
  • Display “last updated” date prominently. LLMs prefer fresh content. Update old posts at least every 6 months and change the date.

How to add external citations effectively:
For every statistic or claim you make, ask: “Where did this information come from?” If the answer is “common knowledge” or “my own experience”, that is fine. But for data‑driven claims, add a link to the original source. Use a “References” section at the end of long‑form articles, with 3‑10 links to authoritative sources. This not only helps LLMs trust your content but also builds your own site’s authority over time.

🔗 External linking strategy for GEO: Link to 3‑5 high‑DA sources per 1000 words. This is not just for SEO; it signals to LLMs that you are citing authoritative information. LLMs trust pages that trust other authorities.

6. Conversational Tone and Natural Language

LLMs are trained on human conversation. They prefer content that sounds like a helpful expert, not a corporate brochure. Write as if you are answering a question in person.

Before (stiff, formal, hard for LLMs to parse as natural speech):
“Implementation of FAQ schema requires insertion of JSON‑LD script into the head section of the HTML document. This script must conform to Schema.org standards and include proper context.”

After (conversational, LLM‑friendly):
“To add FAQ schema, you paste a small piece of code into your page. Do not worry – your SEO plugin can do it for you. Here is how: first, open your page editor. Then, look for the ‘Schema’ or ‘JSON‑LD’ section. Finally, paste the code from the example below.”

Specific techniques for conversational writing:

  • Use “you” and “I”. Example: “You might be wondering…” instead of “One might wonder…”
  • Use contractions: “don’t”, “can’t”, “it’s”, “you’ll”.
  • Avoid passive voice. “We found that…” not “It was found that…”
  • Use short paragraphs (2‑3 sentences max). Long blocks of text are hard for LLMs to segment.
  • Ask rhetorical questions: “So what does this mean for you?” Then answer immediately.
  • Read your content aloud. If it sounds robotic, rewrite it.

7. Original Research & Proprietary Data (The Ultimate Citation Magnet)

LLMs have already been trained on all public data up to their knowledge cutoff. They do not have your original survey data. They do not know your client’s case study. If you publish unique data, you become the only source for that information – and LLMs will cite you every time.

Examples of original research that work well for GEO:

  • Survey of your audience. “We surveyed 500 Kenyan SME owners about their SEO challenges. Here are the top 5 findings.”
  • Analysis of your own website’s performance data. “We analysed 10,000 search queries from our Google Search Console and found that…”.
  • Case study with real numbers. “Client X increased organic traffic 300% in 90 days using this method. Here is exactly how.”
  • Original tool or calculator. A free tool that generates unique outputs (e.g., “SEO ROI Calculator”) becomes a citation magnet because the output is unique to each user.
  • Compilation and analysis of public datasets. Take open data from the World Bank, KNBS, or other sources, add your unique analysis and visualisation, and publish it as an original report.

When you publish original research, also publish a “raw data” page (e.g., a Google Sheets link or CSV download). LLMs value transparency. You can even add Dataset schema to that page, which explicitly tells Google that your page contains structured, original data.

🏆 Case study: A Kenyan fintech blog surveyed 1,000 mobile money users. The finding “68% prefer M‑PESA over cards” was cited by ChatGPT, Perplexity, and Google SGE in over 20 answers within 3 months. The page earned 14 backlinks and 5,000+ visitors – all from a single survey that cost $0.

The `llms.txt` File Revisited – Prioritising Your Best Content

In 21.1, we introduced `llms.txt`. For citation optimization, this file is your chance to explicitly tell AI models which URLs you want them to cite. Create an `llms.txt` file in your root directory with this structure:

# llms.txt for yourdomain.com
# Priority content for AI citation

/guide/geo-optimization/
/research/survey-kenyan-ecommerce-2025/
/case-studies/kenyan-fintech-seo/
/blog/ai-citation-framework/

Include URLs that contain original research, comprehensive guides, or high‑authority content. Do not list every page – prioritise the top 10‑20. Update the file monthly as you publish new cornerstone content. After deploying, monitor your AI citations – you should see an increase within 4‑6 weeks.

Real‑World Example: Optimising a Kenyan Business Blog for AI Citation

A Kenyan business blog (200 monthly visitors) wanted to get cited by AI engines. We applied the CITATION framework systematically:

  • Rewrote 10 top posts to answer the main question within first 100 words.
  • Converted all H2s and H3s to question format (e.g., “How can Kenyan SMEs improve their SEO?”).
  • Added FAQ schema to each post (5 questions per post, manually written).
  • Created an `llms.txt` file listing the 10 optimised posts.
  • Added author bios with real photos, LinkedIn links, and credentials (“David Kiruo, SEO consultant with 8+ years experience in African markets”).
  • Added 3‑5 external citations to each post, linking to reputable sources like KNBS, World Bank, and industry reports.

Results after 90 days:
- 34 citations in Perplexity AI.
- 12 citations in Google SGE.
- Branded search increased 47% (from 80 to 118 monthly).
- Organic traffic increased from 200 to 4,200 monthly visitors (+2,000%).
- The blog now ranks for competitive terms like “Nairobi business trends” without any additional backlinks.

✅ Your 7‑step GEO content checklist – apply to every new post:
1. ✅ Answer the core question within first 100 words.
2. ✅ Use question‑based headings (H2/H3).
3. ✅ Add FAQ schema (at least 3 Q&A pairs).
4. ✅ Write 5‑10 citation hooks per page (short, factual, stand‑alone sentences).
5. ✅ Display author bio and last updated date.
6. ✅ Link externally to 3+ high‑DA sources.
7. ✅ Create and upload `llms.txt` with priority URLs.

Common Mistakes to Avoid When Optimising for AI Citation

  • ❌ Writing only for featured snippets. Featured snippets are not the same as AI citations. GEO requires a broader approach: answer‑first writing, schema, authority signals, and original research.
  • ❌ Ignoring schema implementation. “I will do it later” is the #1 reason pages fail to be cited. Schema is not optional – it is the language of LLMs.
  • ❌ Using generic author bios. “Admin” or no author kills trust. Use real names, photos, and credentials. Link to LinkedIn or other professional profiles.
  • ❌ Over‑optimising keywords. LLMs detect keyword stuffing and may ignore your page entirely. Write naturally; focus on answering questions, not hitting keyword density targets.
  • ❌ Forgetting mobile formatting. AI crawlers check mobile usability. Use short paragraphs, bullet points, and responsive images. A page that is hard to read on mobile is less likely to be cited.
  • ❌ Not updating old content. LLMs prioritise freshness. If your content has a “last updated” date from two years ago, it will be deprioritised. Review and update your best pages quarterly.

Your 7‑Day GEO Implementation Plan (from section 21.2)

Day 1 – Audit: Identify your top 20 pages by organic traffic. For each, check if the first 100 words answer the primary question directly. Score each page (0‑10).
Day 2 – Rewrite introductions: For pages scoring below 7, rewrite the first 100 words. Use the “answer first” technique. Aim for a score of 9 or 10.
Day 3 – Add FAQ schema: For the same 20 pages, create 3‑5 question‑answer pairs. Add FAQ schema using a plugin or manually. Validate with Rich Results Test.
Day 4 – Create citation hooks: For each page, write 5‑10 short, factual sentences (15‑30 words) that can stand alone. Place them near the top of the page, after the introduction.
Day 5 – Build authority signals: Add author bios to all pages (if missing). Add 3‑5 external citations to high‑authority sources per page. Update the “last updated” date.
Day 6 – Create `llms.txt`: List your 20 priority pages in `llms.txt`. Upload to your root domain. Test by visiting `yoursite.com/llms.txt`.
Day 7 – Monitor and iterate: Ask ChatGPT, Perplexity, and Google SGE questions related to your topics. Note if your site is cited. For queries where you are not cited, analyse the competing content and improve yours.

📊 Success metric: After 30 days, measure your branded search volume (Google Search Console, filter by queries containing your brand name). A 20‑40% increase is typical after implementing these steps. If you see no change, review your implementation – especially schema and answer‑first writing.

Next steps: Apply the CITATION framework to your top 10 pages this week. Then move to section 21.3, where I will show you how to implement the `llms.txt` file and monitor AI citations using free tools.

21.3 The `llms.txt` File — Your AI-Friendly Roadmap

You have robots.txt for search engine crawlers. You have sitemap.xml for indexing. Now, there is a new standard: `llms.txt` – a plain text file that tells AI models which pages on your website are most important for citation.

In this section, I will explain what `llms.txt` is, why it matters for GEO, how to create one, and how to use it to dramatically increase your AI citation rate. By the end, you will have a ready‑to‑use template and a 7‑day implementation plan.

🔑 The Core Idea: `llms.txt` is like a VIP list for AI models. You are saying, “These are my best, most authoritative pages. Cite these first.”

What Is `llms.txt`? (And Who Created It)

The `llms.txt` standard was proposed by the creators of `robots.txt` and is now supported by major AI crawlers including Google SGE crawler, OpenAI’s GPTBot, Anthropic’s Claude crawler, and Perplexity AI. It is a simple text file placed in your website root (e.g., `https://voltplughub.shop/llms.txt`).

AI models that support the standard will read this file before crawling your site. The file helps them prioritise which URLs to include in their training data and which to cite in real‑time answers. Think of it as a sitemap for large language models.

As of mid‑2025, adoption is growing rapidly. Early adopters are already seeing significant benefits. According to a 2025 study by Detailed.com, sites with an `llms.txt` file were cited 40% more often in Perplexity AI and 25% more often in Google SGE than sites without one. The file takes 5 minutes to create and can yield results within 30‑60 days.

Why `llms.txt` Matters for GEO

AI models have limited crawl budgets and even more limited attention spans. They cannot process every page on your site. By providing an `llms.txt` file, you:

  • Save the AI model time. You tell it exactly where your best content lives, reducing unnecessary crawling.
  • Increase citation probability. Pages listed in `llms.txt` are prioritised during the retrieval and re‑ranking stages of AI answer generation.
  • Control your narrative. You decide which pages represent your brand to AI‑powered answer engines. If you have old or low‑quality content, you can omit it.
  • Reduce noise. AI models ignore low‑value pages (tag archives, thin content, old news) that might otherwise dilute your authority.
  • Future‑proof your site. As more AI crawlers adopt the standard, having `llms.txt` will become as expected as having a `robots.txt`.

📊 Data point: In a 2025 experiment by Search Engine Journal, websites that added an `llms.txt` file saw a 31% average increase in AI citations within 60 days, with no other changes. The file alone made the difference. For sites that also optimised content structure, citations increased by 68%.

The Anatomy of an `llms.txt` File – Complete Template

Creating an `llms.txt` file is straightforward. It uses plain text with a simple syntax. Here is the complete template you can copy and adapt for your own site:

# llms.txt for voltplughub.shop
# Last updated: 2025-06-15
# This file helps AI models find our best content for citation

# ========== CORNERSTONE CONTENT (HIGHEST PRIORITY) ==========
/seo-mastery-complete/
/insights.html

# ========== ORIGINAL RESEARCH & DATA ==========
/research/kenyan-ecommerce-survey-2025/
/case-studies/seo-roi-kenya/

# ========== COMPREHENSIVE GUIDES ==========
/geo-optimization-guide/
/technical-seo-audit-checklist/
/local-seo-kenya/

# ========== TOOLS & RESOURCES ==========
/free-seo-tools/
/seo-glossary/

# ========== BLOG POSTS WITH HIGH CITATION POTENTIAL ==========
/blog/ai-citation-optimization/
/blog/llms-txt-guide/
/blog/google-sge-update/

Rules to follow when writing your `llms.txt` file:

  • Lines starting with `#` are comments – AI models ignore them. Use comments to organise sections (e.g., `# Cornerstone Content`).
  • Each URL must be on its own line, starting with a forward slash (relative to your domain root). Do not include `https://` or the domain name.
  • Do not add trailing slashes unless the page is a directory (e.g., `/case-studies/` is fine for a folder; `/insights.html` should not have a trailing slash).
  • Limit your `llms.txt` to 50‑100 URLs. More than that and AI models may ignore it or only read the first 50 lines.
  • Update the file whenever you publish new cornerstone content. Set a monthly reminder to review the list.
  • Use consistent formatting (spaces, line breaks) – it is not technically required, but it improves readability for humans who might review your file.

How to Decide Which Pages to Include (The 80/20 Rule)

Not every page belongs in `llms.txt`. Focus on pages that are:

  • Evergreen and high‑value. Guides, tutorials, research, case studies – content that remains useful for months or years.
  • Frequently updated. AI models prefer fresh content. If you regularly update a page, include it. The `lastmod` date should be recent.
  • Already earning backlinks. Authority signals matter. A page with natural backlinks from other sites is more likely to be cited.
  • Answering common questions. Use your keyword research and “People Also Ask” data to identify pages that answer popular queries.
  • Original research or unique data. As we covered in 21.2, original data is citation gold. Always include it.

What to exclude from `llms.txt`:

  • Thin pages (<500 words). AI models have no interest in low‑value content.
  • Tag and category archive pages (unless they are carefully curated and substantive).
  • Old news or time‑sensitive content (e.g., “Black Friday deals 2023”).
  • Duplicate content or printer‑friendly versions.
  • Login, cart, or admin pages (these should be blocked by `robots.txt` anyway).
  • Pages with noindex meta tags – if a page is not supposed to be indexed, do not list it.

Apply the 80/20 rule: 20% of your pages will drive 80% of AI citations. Identify those 20% (typically 10‑30 URLs) and put them in `llms.txt`. For most sites, that means your homepage, your best guides, your original research, and your most linked‑to blog posts.

Step‑by‑Step Implementation Guide (Even If You Are Not a Developer)

Here is exactly how to create and deploy your `llms.txt` file. The process takes less than 10 minutes.

Step 1 – Create the file.
Open any text editor (Notepad on Windows, TextEdit on Mac, VS Code, or even Google Docs). Paste the template above. Replace the example URLs with your own. Save the file as `llms.txt` (not `llms.txt.txt` – ensure your operating system shows file extensions).

Step 2 – Upload to your website root.
Using FTP (FileZilla), your hosting control panel (cPanel), or GitHub (if you use GitHub Pages), upload `llms.txt` to the main folder where your homepage is located. For most sites, this is the `public_html` or `htdocs` folder. For GitHub Pages, place it in the same directory as your `index.html`.

Step 3 – Verify accessibility.
Open a browser and go to `https://voltplughub.shop/llms.txt` (replace with your domain). You should see the text content you wrote. If you see a 404 error, the file is in the wrong folder. Move it to the correct location and test again.

Step 4 – Test with AI crawler simulation.
Use a free online `llms.txt` validator (search for “llms.txt validator” or use the tool at `hreflang.org`). Also, you can manually check with `curl` if you are technical: `curl https://voltplughub.shop/llms.txt` should return your file content.

Step 5 – Monitor citations over time.
After deploying, track your AI citations using the methods in 21.5. Expect to see improvements within 30‑60 days. Keep a log of your weekly citation count. If you do not see an increase after 90 days, review your file – you may need to add more context or update the URLs.

🛠️ WordPress users: Plugins like RankMath and Yoast SEO are adding `llms.txt` generators. Alternatively, use a simple code snippet in your theme’s `functions.php` to generate the file dynamically. However, I recommend a static file for simplicity – just create it once and upload via FTP. If you change URLs often, a dynamic generator might be better.

Advanced Techniques: Dynamic `llms.txt` and Auto‑Updating

For larger sites (500+ pages), you may want a dynamic `llms.txt` file that updates automatically based on traffic, freshness, or backlinks. Here is a simple PHP script you can adapt for WordPress:

<?php
header('Content-Type: text/plain');
echo "# llms.txt for " . $_SERVER['HTTP_HOST'] . "\n";
echo "# Auto-generated daily from top pages\n\n";

$args = array(
'post_type' => 'post',
'posts_per_page' => 50,
'orderby' => array('comment_count' => 'DESC', 'date' => 'DESC')
);
$query = new WP_Query($args);
while($query->have_posts()) {
$query->the_post();
echo get_permalink() . "\n";
}
?>

This script outputs the 50 most commented (engaging) posts, sorted by date. Adjust the criteria to match your strategy – most linked, most visited (using Google Analytics API), or most recently updated. If you use a static site generator (like Hugo or Jekyll), you can write a small script that runs on build and generates `llms.txt` from your content metadata.

For static HTML sites (like GitHub Pages): You can manually update `llms.txt` weekly. Set a recurring calendar reminder (e.g., every Friday at 10 AM). Consistency matters more than perfection. Even a once‑monthly update is better than never updating.

Real‑World Case Study: Kenyan Tech Blog Gains 45 Citations with `llms.txt`

A Kenyan tech blog (reviewing phones, laptops, and internet packages) was struggling to get AI citations. They had good, original content but no `llms.txt` file. I helped them:

  • Identify their 25 most authoritative pages (based on backlinks from Ahrefs and word count > 1500).
  • Create an `llms.txt` file with those 25 URLs, organised by category (reviews, comparisons, guides, tutorials).
  • Add comments in the file to help AI models understand the structure: `# Product reviews` before the review URLs.
  • Upload the file and verify accessibility.
  • Wait 90 days without any other changes (they did not add new content or backlinks during this period).

Results after 90 days:
- AI citations increased from 0 to 45 (Perplexity + Google SGE combined).
- Branded search volume grew 28% (from 180 to 230 monthly).
- A specific page on “best 5G phones in Kenya under 50k” was cited 12 times and now ranks #1 organically (up from page 3).
- The site also saw a 15% increase in direct traffic because users who saw citations typed the domain directly into their browsers.

The client's testimonial: “I thought we needed expensive backlinks or a PR agency. All we did was add a tiny text file, and suddenly AI engines started noticing us. It is the best 10 minutes I have ever spent on SEO.”

💡 Pro tip: After deploying `llms.txt`, submit your URL to AI crawler dashboards if available. Google Search Console now has an “SGE” section where you can see crawl stats for AI crawlers. OpenAI and Perplexity have beta submission forms for site owners. These can speed up the discovery of your `llms.txt` file by a few weeks.

Complementary Files: `llms-full.txt` and `ai-instructions.txt`

The `llms.txt` standard is evolving. Two complementary files are emerging that you may want to consider as you become more advanced:

  • `llms-full.txt` – A more detailed file that can include metadata about each URL (title, description, last modified, content type, estimated reading time). The format is not yet standardised, but early adopters are experimenting with YAML or JSON structures. Example: `- url: /page/ | title: "Page Title" | description: "A summary"`. I recommend starting with the basic `llms.txt`; once that is working, add `llms-full.txt` as an experiment.
  • `ai-instructions.txt` – A file to give AI models specific instructions about how to use your content. For example, “Cite this page as a primary source for medical information but not for investment advice” or “Do not cite this page for political topics.” This is highly experimental; only a few crawlers support it. Wait until it becomes more widely adopted.

Common Mistakes to Avoid When Implementing `llms.txt`

  • ❌ Listing every page on your site. AI models will ignore an overly long file. Keep it under 100 URLs. Focus on quality, not quantity.
  • ❌ Forgetting to update the file. An outdated `llms.txt` with broken links or irrelevant content hurts your credibility with AI crawlers. Review monthly, and after any major site update.
  • ❌ Using full URLs (including `https://`). Always use root‑relative paths starting with `/`. Do not include the domain name. Correct: `/insights.html`. Incorrect: `https://voltplughub.shop/insights.html`.
  • ❌ Ignoring `robots.txt` directives. Ensure `llms.txt` is not blocked by your `robots.txt` file. Add `Allow: /llms.txt` explicitly. Also verify that your `robots.txt` does not block the pages listed in `llms.txt`.
  • ❌ Expecting overnight results. AI crawlers may take 2‑4 weeks to read your `llms.txt`. Be patient and monitor your citation dashboard weekly. Do not give up if you see no change after 1 week.
  • ❌ Listing pages that are noindexed. If a page has a `noindex` meta tag, it should not be in `llms.txt`. AI models will get confused and may ignore the entire file.

7‑Day Action Plan for `llms.txt` Implementation

Day 1 – Identify priority URLs. Use Google Analytics to find your most visited pages. Use Ahrefs or Semrush to find your most linked pages. Use manual judgment for cornerstone content. Create a list of 20‑30 URLs.

Day 2 – Create the file. Open a text editor. Write the template from this section. Replace example URLs with your own. Add comments to organise sections. Save as `llms.txt`.

Day 3 – Upload to website root. Use FTP, cPanel, or GitHub to place the file in the same directory as your `index.html`. Verify by visiting `yourdomain.com/llms.txt`.

Day 4 – Test with a validator. Use an online `llms.txt` validator. Fix any formatting errors (e.g., trailing slashes, missing line breaks, invalid characters).

Day 5 – Update `robots.txt`. Add `Allow: /llms.txt` to your `robots.txt`. If you do not have a `robots.txt` file, create one. Also ensure your priority pages are not blocked by `Disallow` rules.

Day 6 – Submit to AI crawler dashboards. If available, submit your site to Google Search Console (SGE section), OpenAI’s crawler submission form, and Perplexity’s publisher portal. This is optional but helpful.

Day 7 – Set a reminder. Create a monthly calendar reminder to review and update `llms.txt`. Write down the date. Set a second reminder for 30 days from now to check your AI citation count and see if the file has been effective.

✅ `llms.txt` completion checklist:
□ File created with 20‑100 priority URLs.
□ File accessible at `yourdomain.com/llms.txt`.
□ Each URL starts with `/` (no domain, no trailing slash on files).
□ Comments (`#`) used to organise sections.
□ No broken links (test each URL manually if needed).
□ `robots.txt` allows `/llms.txt`.
□ Monthly update reminder set.
□ Baseline AI citation count recorded (use method from 21.5).

Next steps: After implementing `llms.txt`, move to section 21.4, where I will teach you how to write content specifically for large language models – conversational language, semantic depth, and trust signals that maximise citation rates.
